    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="b1166ddf45e0446e8be054e3adb511ea">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'c8696c1e-2068-4b6c-8ec9-a2222fc1d001'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

aria-controls="pane-pcw-references" role="menuitem" data-slide-target="#pane-pcw-references" class="tab__nav__item__link article-row-right__tabLinks w-slide__btn references-tab cloned-with-ids empty"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-chain"></i><h2>References</h2></a></li><li role="presentation" class="tab__nav__item"><a id="pane-pcw-relatedcon-cloned" href="#pane-pcw-related" data-slide-clone="self" aria-controls="pane-pcw-related" role="menuitem" data-slide-target="#pane-pcw-related" class="tab__nav__item__link article-row-right__tabLinks w-slide__btn related-tab    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d44637607" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The pursuit of high-performance batteries has propelled the investigation into advanced materials and design methodologies. Herein, the yolk-shell MnSe/ZnSe heterojunction encapsulated in hollow carbontubes (MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs) is prepared as a prospective electrode material for sodium/potassium batteries. The band structure in the heterojunction is methodically adjusted and regulated by intentionally utilizing Mn with unpaired electrons in the 3d orbital. The ZnSe shell confer effectively mitigates volumetric expansion challenges inherent in ions insertion/extraction processes and 1D carbontubular conductive substrate avert the aggregation of MnSe/ZnSe nanoparticles. Concurrently, the heterojunctions implantation induces sublattice distortion and charge redistribution, enriching active sites and regulating band structure. The selenium vacancies within these heterojunctions contribute to the provision of abundant active sites, thereby promoting efficient ions insertion/extraction. In sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs present a superior capacity of 475&nbsp;mA&nbsp;hg<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and sustains a capacity of 408.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even after 1000 cycles. In potassium-ion batteries (KIBs), MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs deliver a higher specific capacity of 422&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and maintain a high coulombic efficiency of 99% after 1000 cycles. The yolk-shell structured MnSe/ZnSe heterojunction demonstrates excellent electrode properties for high-performance sodium/potassium batteries, holding significant promise for future energy storage applications.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307747-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307747-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>To date, the demand for high-performance energy storage systems has grown significantly, primarily driven by the increasing adoption of electric vehicles and renewable energy technologies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0001_R_d44637599e391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Small">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although lithium-ion batteries maintain their dominance in energy storage, the limited availability and rising costs of lithium resources have promoted the requirement for alternative electrode materials with larger reservoirs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0002_R_d44637599e396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this background, SIBs and KIBs have emerged as a viable alternative to the existing lithium-ion batteries due to their inherent abundance and cost-effectiveness of sodium and potassium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0003_R_d44637599e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Mater. Today">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the pursuit of efficient and enduring electrode materials for sodium-ion and potassium-ion batteries confronts enduring challenges. Alleviating these limitations has prompted in-depth, multifaceted research efforts and an unwavering commitment to innovate new electrode materials with improved electrochemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0004_R_d44637599e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Commun.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The progression of SIBs and KIBs technologies hinges critically on the meticulous development of electrode materials that simultaneously epitomize efficiency and stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0005_R_d44637599e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Electrochim. Acta">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To achieve higher performance batteries, these electrode materials should embody increased specific capacity, superior rate performance, long-term cycle durability, and economic viability. Furthermore, the most important considerations are the exploration of innovative materials and groundbreaking design strategies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0006_R_d44637599e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Alloy. Compd.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This urgent need is in response to the unique challenges posed by the relatively large ion size and inherently higher reactivity of sodium and potassium ions relative to lithium ions. Among the electrode materials, manganese dioxide (MnO<sub>2</sub>) has conspicuously emerged as a sanguine contender due to its theoretical specific capacity, compelling cost-efficiency, environmentally benign disposition, and commendable electrochemical stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0007_R_d44637599e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Eng. J.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, MnO<sub>2</sub> encounters a notable constraint in the realm of modest electrical conductivity. This hindered the unobstructed movement of electrons within the intricate network of the electrode matrix. Transition metal selenides have garnered extensive acclaim as prime electrode materials for achieving high-performance batteries due to their impressive specific capacity and their utilization of the conversion reaction mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0008_R_d44637599e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Particularly noteworthy are transition metal selenides such as MoSe<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0009_R_d44637599e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CoSe<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0010_R_d44637599e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Int. J. Energy Res.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and WSe<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0011_R_d44637599e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Small">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which have attracted considerable attention owing to their relatively narrow bandgap, enhanced electrical conductivity, substantial theoretical capacity, robust redox reversibility, and weakened metal-selenium (Se) bonds that facilitate favorable transformation reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0012_R_d44637599e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Cell Rep Phys Sci">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with transition metal oxides/sulfides, transition metal selenides enhance the insertion and extraction capabilities of metal ions. Manganese selenide (MnSe) is considered as a notably auspicious candidate material for SIBs and KIBs due to its high theoretical specific capacities, large interlayer spacing, fast redox kinetics, and good rate capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0013_R_d44637599e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, MnSe undergoes low conductivity, particle aggregation, and drastic volume changes during charging/discharging process. These complexities significantly degrade the cycling stability of electrode materials and causes safety issues of the batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0014_R_d44637599e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To address these above challenges of MnSe, several strategies have been explored, including nanoscale engineering, binder optimization, and structural modifications. The concept of constructing a yolk-shell structure has emerged as an effective approach. Yolk-shell has abundant porous structure, it can promote electrolyte penetration and shorten the ion diffusion path. Meanwhile, the shell provides sufficient void space and a highly adaptive structure, ensuring high electrode stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0015_R_d44637599e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This design, exemplified by Yang et&nbsp;al., involves the formation of yolk-shell composites, as evidenced by their study on Anatase@TiO<sub>2</sub>(B) spheres, where a yolk-shell composite uniformly incorporating both materials exhibited an improved specific capacity of 91.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 375 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0016_R_d44637599e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Power Sources">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In yolk-shell architectures, the material serves as the source of desired electrochemical activity, while the shell material contributes to stability and structural integrity. This synergistic effect yields enhanced ion diffusion, reduced side reactions, and an overall improved performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0017_R_d44637599e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Electrochim. Acta">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Importantly, engineering MnSe in combination with other compounds can create heterogeneous interfaces between components, potentially yielding unique advantages not achievable with carbon-based composites. The formation of the heterojunction generates an inherent electric field, thereby enhancing the charge transfer process and inhibiting electron-hole recombination.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0018_R_d44637599e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition to traditional approaches, novel packaging strategies are also showing application prospect.</p>
         
         <p>In addition, 1D cable-like encapsulation has emerged as an alternative, offering benefits such as heightened electronic conductivity, establishment of charge migration networks, and improved structural integrity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0019_R_d44637599e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Materials">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, the synthesis of a N-doped carbon fibers@SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoflowers (NC@SnO<sub>2</sub>) composite via electrospinning/hydrothermal methods, which showed a reversible discharge capacity of 270&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0020_R_d44637599e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano-Micro Lett.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The inherent properties of 1D carbon, including excellent electrical conductivity, mechanical strength, and chemical stability, contribute to enhanced electrochemical performance. Moreover, the tubular structure of 1D carbon serves as a confined space, which can effectively prevent the aggregation and pulverization of active materials and enhance the cycling stability of the electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0016_R_d44637599e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Power Sources">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Dandelion is an abundant and natural biomass, and its tomentum can be transformed into a conductive carbontubular structure via facile carbonization. The carbontubular structure provides more ion transmission paths, which can buffer the large volume expansion/contraction of the grown nanomaterials during charging/discharging process. Therefore, it is a promising conductive substrate for the selenides.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we designed and synthesized a yolk-shell MnSe/ZnSe heterojunction uniformly embedded in 1D carbontubes (HCTs) as anode materials of SIBs and KIBs. The yolk-shell structured MnSe/ZnSe heterostructure can facilitate the insertion/extraction of sodium ions and create a gradient of the chemical potential. The selenium vacancy within the MnSe/ZnSe heterojunctions as an in-plane channel for ions diffusion through the material, it is beneficial to the molecular dynamics of ions intercalation. Moreover, the ZnSe shell offers improved stability and mitigates the volume expansion issues often encountered during sodium and potassium insertion/extraction processes. Additionally, the utilization of 1D carbontubular conductive substrate effectively ensures the well-dispersed nanoparticles as well as promotes efficient electron transport. As a electrode for SIBs, MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs exhibited an excellent capacity of 475&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and an ultrastable cycle capacity of 408.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles. In KIBs, MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs showed a specific capacity of 422&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and maintain a high coulombic efficiency of 99% after 1000 cycles. The unique combination of materials and the synergistic effects between the yolk-shell structure and the carbon nanotube host matrix are expected to provide superior electrochemical performance, highlighting the potential of this design for future energy storage applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307747-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307747-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The synthesis process of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs is presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0001">1a</a>, and detailed procedures can be found in the experimental section. Initially, MnSe/ZnSe yolk-shell heterojunctions were deposited within the interstices of carbontubes via simple solvothermal and selenization methods, then the unique yolk-shell MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs was obtained. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0001">1b</a> showcases the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of both the synthesized MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs with encapsulation-type structure and the MnSe@HCTs reference. The discernible peaks in the XRD pattern of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs correspondingly align with hexagonal ZnSe (JCPDS No. 15–0105) and MnSe (JCPDS No. 11–0683), affirming the establishment of the MnSe/ZnSe heterostructure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0021_R_d44637599e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Ionics">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Significantly, ZnSe exhibited distinct diffraction peaks situated at 28°, 32°, 47°, 55°, 58°, 68°, 78°, and 87°, which corresponded meticulously to the crystalline facets of (111), (200), (220), (311), (222), (400), (420), and (422) within the cubic α-MnSe lattice (JCPDS: 11–0683). Importantly, amid the diverse phases observed in MnSe (namely, α-MnSe, β-MnSe, and γ-MnSe), it is noteworthy that α-MnSe exhibits the most elevated thermodynamic stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0022_R_d44637599e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Mater. Today Energy">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, ZnSe exhibits eight distinct diffraction peaks positioned at 25.95°, 27.42°, 29.25°, 37.97°, 45.38°, 49.50°, 53.78°, and 69.41°, corresponding to the lattice planes of (100), (002), (101), (102), (110), (103), (112), and (203).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0023_R_d44637599e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Significantly, the presence of HCTs was postulated to significantly contribute to the encapsulation and formation of MnSe/ZnSe heterostructures, specifically influencing the expansion of the (200) crystal planes. The structural characteristics of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs were investigated through scanning electron microscopes (SEM) and transmission electron microscopes (TEM) at varying magnifications. The integrity of the hollow tubular morphology of HCTs was well-preserved (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Further refinements led to the development of spatially confined MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0001">1d</a>). This configuration can enhance specific capacity by accommodating a greater weight ratio of active components due to the porous nature of the structure. TEM analysis of MnSe@HCTs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0001">1e</a>) and MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0001">1g</a>) demonstrated the uniform distribution of the yolk-shell MnSe/ZnSe heterostructure on the surface of HCTs. The microstructural confirmation was reinforced through high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images of MnSe@HCTs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0001">1f</a>) and MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0001">1h</a>), revealing interplanar spacings of 0.19&nbsp;nm and 0.27&nbsp;nm, in alignment with the (220) and (200) crystal planes of α-MnSe, respectively, corroborating XRD findings.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0024_R_d44637599e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Mater. Lett.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The distinct phase boundary between ZnSe and MnSe is clearly discernible as shown by the yellow line, thus confirming the successful construction of the yolk-shell structure in MnSe/ZnSe@HCT. Energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) analysis of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs exhibited the uniform distribution of elements C, Se, Mn, and Zn (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0001">1i</a>), further validating the effective formation of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs. Notably, the distribution patterns of Mn, Se, and Zn elements are in harmony with that of C, emphasizing the uniform growth of heterostructured MnSe/ZnSe nanosheets on HCT.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0025_R_d44637599e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Eng. J.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307747-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5b999013-e4de-4cd4-afd2-2a4c58b14ac6/smll202307747-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5b999013-e4de-4cd4-afd2-2a4c58b14ac6/smll202307747-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/79cf1ab3-e7f2-443b-b150-22dea9b81877/smll202307747-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5b999013-e4de-4cd4-afd2-2a4c58b14ac6/smll202307747-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307747-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307747" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis process for MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs composite. b) XRD patterns of the materials. SEM images of c) MnSe@HCTs and d) MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs composites. e) TEM image and f) HRTEM image of MnSe@HCTs. g) TEM image and h) HRTEM image of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs. i) EDS mapping images of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The Fourier-transform infrared (FTIR) spectra (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0002">2a</a>) of both MnSe@HCTs material and MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs material manifest distinct absorption bands that are associated with carbonaceous materials. These features encompass the C─H stretching vibrational mode situated at ≈2900&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, as well as the C═C stretching vibrational mode occurring ≈1600&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, the FTIR spectrum of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs material manifests absorption peaks at approximately 1200&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1500&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, attributed to the stretching vibrations of Se─C and Se─Se bonds, respectively, inherent to the MnSe and ZnSe constituents.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0026_R_d44637599e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nanotechnology">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the Raman spectra of MnSe@HCTs material and MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0002">2b</a>), the characteristic peaks corresponding to MnSe and ZnSe can be identified respectively. Specifically, the Raman spectrum of MnSe@HCTs showed three principal peaks, each associated with distinct vibrational modes. In MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs, a prominent peak emerged at around 241&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the A<sub>1g</sub> mode and signifying in-plane vibration of Se atoms. Another peak at approximately 285&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponded to the E<sub>2g</sub> mode, indicative of out-of-plane vibration of Se atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0027_R_d44637599e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Alloy. Compd.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs exhibited three distinct Raman scattering lines at ≈154, 258, and 486&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, with the 154&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> line attributed to hexagonal Se, consistent with previous analyses.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0028_R_d44637599e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Phys. Rev. B">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) spectra was employed to gain a comprehensive understanding of the defect characteristics inherent in the prepared material, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0002">2c</a>. The distinctive EPR signal observed at g&nbsp;=&nbsp;2.003 was indicative of unpaired electrons confined within the selenium vacancies within the samples. Notably, MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs exhibited higher pronounced EPR signal than that of MnSe@HCTs. The emergence of defects can be attributed to both the re-coordination of Mn and the cleavage of Zn coordination bonds during the ion exchange process. Furthermore, the presence of Mn<sup>II</sup> (d<sup>3</sup>) contributed to the heightened formation of unpaired electrons within MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs. For the determination of carbon content in as-prepared MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs, a thermal gravity analysis (TGA) was employed (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). TGA of both MnSe@HCTs and MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs were conducted from room temperature to 800&nbsp;°C. The curves exhibited significant weight loss within the 330–600&nbsp;°C temperature range, corresponding to the volatilization of SeO<sub>2</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub>. Notably, the major weight loss between 350 and 450&nbsp;°C aligns with the sublimation temperature of selenium dioxide, while the subsequent 450–550&nbsp;°C interval signifies carbon combustion. The mass percentages of carbon content are calculated as 55% for MnSe@HCTs and 49.2% for MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs, corroborated by the TGA curves.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0029_R_d44637599e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Cryst. Growth">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) technique was employed to analyze the elemental composition and chemical states of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs. The survey spectrum of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0002">2d</a>) exhibited the presence of Mn, Zn, Se, and C, consistent with the previous EDS characterizations. The ratio of Zn and Mn elements in MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs calculated from XPS spectra is 1.65:1.37 (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Further insights into valence states and element binding were derived from high-resolution spectra. Specifically, XPS analysis elucidated the chemical compositions and surface electronic states, focusing on Mn 2p and Se 3d regions. Concurring with previous findings,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0030_R_d44637599e708" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nanomaterials">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the peaks at 654.0 and 641.8&nbsp;eV corresponded to Mn<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Mn<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively, indicative of the Mn-ion positive bivalent charge. Correspondingly, binding energies of 56.1 and 55.3&nbsp;eV were attributed to 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and 3d<sub>5/2</sub>, representing the presence of Se<sup>2−</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0031_R_d44637599e728" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Ionics">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of oxidized Se was manifested by the peak at 58.7&nbsp;eV.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0032_R_d44637599e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater. Technol.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In summation, the chemical composition predominantly comprised Mn<sup>2+</sup> and Se<sup>2−</sup>, aligning with the standard MnSe valence state. The C 1s spectra for both samples revealed two discernible peaks at around 284.5 and 288.0&nbsp;eV, corresponding to graphitic carbon and carbon atoms in functional groups, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0033_R_d44637599e743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further probing the chemical states, Zn 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Zn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> binding energies were located at 1021.4 and 1044.5&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0002">2i</a>), representing the presence of Zn<sup>2+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0034_R_d44637599e758" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Mater. Sci.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This XPS analysis provides comprehensive insights into the elemental and chemical characteristics of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307747-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/28703bac-f7ed-4e14-8927-f7a343a871c3/smll202307747-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/28703bac-f7ed-4e14-8927-f7a343a871c3/smll202307747-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/95756ded-2d98-4226-9f22-dc051103de83/smll202307747-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/28703bac-f7ed-4e14-8927-f7a343a871c3/smll202307747-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307747-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307747" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Raman spectra, b) FTIR spectra and c) EPR spectra of MnSe@HCTs and MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs. d) XPS survey spectra of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs. High-resolution Mn 2p spectrum of e) MnSe@HCTs and f) MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs. High-resolution Se 3d spectrum of g) MnSe@HCTs and h) MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs. i) Zn 2p spectrum of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">This study delved into an in-depth exploration of the electrochemical characteristics inherent to nanostructured MnSe@HCTs (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) and MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0003">3</a>), employed as promising anode materials within the domain of SIBs. This exploration aimed to elucidate the sodiation mechanism and the reversibility of the electrochemical reactions. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0003">3a</a> illustrates the cyclic voltammetry (CV) profiles of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs at varying scan rates. In the first cycle, a pair of discrete cathodic peaks appeared at 1.12 and 0.54&nbsp;V, mainly attributed to the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and the simultaneous establishment of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) produced by the electrolyte decomposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0011_R_d44637599e799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Small">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon the anodic sweep, a distinct peak materialized at 1.26&nbsp;V was closely correlating the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions from Na<sub>2</sub>Se. Additionally,&nbsp;a minor oxidation peak located at 1.52&nbsp;V, demonstrating the deintercalation process and consequent formation of MnSe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0035_R_d44637599e808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Electrochim. Acta">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the current intensity of the 1.12&nbsp;V peak increases throughout the test, indicative of a potential activation process within the electrode, potentially contributing to the enhanced specific capacity of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs. Subsequent scans exhibited two cathodic peaks at 2.15 and 1.86&nbsp;V, signifying Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation forming Na<sub>x</sub>MnSe, progressing to conversion into Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Mn.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0036_R_d44637599e820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Commun.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, ≈0&nbsp;V, a pair of redox peaks arise, attributed to Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation within the carbon layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0037_R_d44637599e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nat. Commun.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The negligible deviation of peak potential and peak intensity observed starting from the second cycle demonstrated good electrochemical reversibility and long-lasting stability of SIBs. The augmented electrochemical efficacy demonstrated by MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs can be ascribed to the synergistic interplay between MnSe and ZnSe inherent within the heterostructure. This synergism was further complemented by the porous architectural design and the homogeneous dispersion of active constituents upon the support matrix of HCTs. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0003">3b</a> presented the galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves for the initial five cycles of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs material, aligning with the oxidation/reduction peaks observed in the CV analysis. MnSe@HCTs (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a>, Supporting Information) exhibited an oxidation peak at approximately 1.08&nbsp;V and a reduction peak around 0.68&nbsp;V, corresponding to MnSe redox reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0031_R_d44637599e839" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Ionics">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs material displayed oxidation and reduction peaks at approximately 1.02 and 0.75&nbsp;V, respectively, attributed to both ZnSe and MnSe redox reactions. The initial cycle registers discharge and charge capacities of 835 and 508&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, resulting in a coulombic efficiency (CE) of 60.8%. This initial capacity loss is primarily attributed to SEI film formation and electrolyte decomposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0038_R_d44637599e847" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Carbon">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequent cycles maintain a discharge capacity ≈498&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, yielding CEs approaching 99.9%. MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs material exhibited superior specific capacity and cycling stability due to the collaborative impact of ZnSe and MnSe, which offered increased active sites for electrochemical reactions and heightened structural stability during charge/discharge cycles. Furthermore, exceptional rate capabilities across diverse current rates from 0.1 to 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> were observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0003">3c</a>), demonstrating stable capacities at each rate. Notably, the sample achieved a substantial capacity of ≈220&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating efficient Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and high electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0039_R_d44637599e866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Power Sources">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Capacity fully recovers upon the reduction of the current rate to 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, signifying commendable reversibility. The HCTs also showed a good rate performance as conductive substrate (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information). A comprehensive exploration of the dynamic Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism was undertaken through a quantitative analysis employing CV over a range of scan rates spanning from 0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. The resulting profiles, depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0003">3d</a>, exhibit an elevation in current density alongside heightened scan rates. Notably, the nonlinearity between peak current and the square root of scan rate (<i>v<sup>1/2</sup></i>) presented&nbsp;the involvement of both diffusion-controlled reactions and capacitive processes in the sodium storage mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0040_R_d44637599e889" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Small">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, a deep understanding of the charge storage mechanism requires the use of relevant formulas based on the CV curves:

            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202307747-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/fa0946a6-e092-4091-8f35-ee18458912a1/smll202307747-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202307747:smll202307747-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202307747-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">p</mi>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>av</mi>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">b</mi>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{\mathrm{i}}}_{\mathrm{p}} = {\mathrm{av}}^{\mathrm{b}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>i<sub>p</sub></i> (A) denoted the peak current, <i>v</i> (V/s) represented the scan rate, “<i>a</i>” standed as a constant parameter, and the determination of the b-value involved leveraging the linear correlation between <i>i<sub>p</sub></i> and <i>v</i>. Typically, recombination capabilities typically resulted from the confluence of diffusion-controlled (b-value&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.5) and surface-controlled (b-value&nbsp;=&nbsp;1) dynamics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0041_R_d44637599e929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the prevailing influenced governing battery performance can be discerned by examining the b-value. Furthermore, the linear regression curves were presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0003">3f</a>. The b-values of the anodic and cathodic peaks inferred from CV curves containing different scan rates were determined to be 0.85 and 0.88, respectively. These b-values emphasized the concurrent prevalence of electrochemical reactions characterized by both diffusion-controlled and surface-controlled mechanisms within the MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0042_R_d44637599e938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For a further quantitative assessment of the charge storage from the diffusion-controlled and surface-controlled behaviors, the ensuing formula was utilized:

            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202307747-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7f0119cd-013f-4a6c-8b36-8b945d251b4f/smll202307747-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202307747:smll202307747-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/smll202307747-math-0002.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mrow>
                              <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                              <mi>V</mi>
                              <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
                           </mrow>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">k</mi>
                              <mn>1</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>k</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                                 <mo>/</mo>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </mrow>

                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i(V) = {{\mathrm{k}}}_1v + {k}_2{v}^{1/2}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307747-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c6c46d86-dfff-47d8-8e60-7e334b862577/smll202307747-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c6c46d86-dfff-47d8-8e60-7e334b862577/smll202307747-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8860af7c-33ef-4159-acc3-61aff14b2a99/smll202307747-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c6c46d86-dfff-47d8-8e60-7e334b862577/smll202307747-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307747-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307747" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves and b) galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs in Na-ion batteries for the first five cycles. c) Rate performance of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs at a series of current densities from 0.1 to 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. d) CV curves of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs at different scan rates (0.2–1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>). e) The area of capacitance contribution in the CV curves at 0.6&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. f) Logi−Logν curves of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs. g) Contribution ratio of diffusion-controlled and surface-controlled behaviors of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs. h) EIS images of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs and MnSe@HCTs. i) The cycling stability of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs in Na-ion batteries.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In this equation, the comprehensive current encompasses both surface-controlled capacitive (<i>k<sub>1</sub>v</i>) and diffusion-controlled (<i>k<sub>2</sub>v<sup>1/2</sup></i>) behaviors. The twofold aspects of capacity contribution behaviors were quantified by determining the respective values of <i>k<sub>1</sub></i> and <i>k<sub>2</sub></i>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0043_R_d44637599e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Energy Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The determination of the <i>k<sub>1</sub></i>-value entails analyzing the linear relationship between the square root of the scan rate (<i>v<sup>1/2</sup></i>) and the reciprocal of the square root of the current (<i>iv<sup>−1/2</sup></i>). For a scan rate of 0.6&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, the characterization of surface-controlled behaviors were achieved through a multipoint calculation methodology (as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0003">3e</a>). The assessment revealed that the integral area ratio to the total area of the CV curve amounts to 75.8%, thereby highlighting a relatively pronounced contribution from capacitive behavior. Notably, MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs exhibited an escalating capacitive contribution ratio with increasing scan rates, thereby enhancing their rate capability. This trend was illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0003">3g</a>, where the corresponding capacitive behavior contributions ranged from ≈68.8% to 88.5% for scan rates ranging from 0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. The quantitative analysis distinctly confirmed the dominant role of capacitive behavior, indicating swift Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0044_R_d44637599e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Mater. Chem.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For a further understanding of the effects attributed to the distinct structure, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was conducted to scrutinize the reaction kinetics of both MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs and the reference MnSe@HCTs anodes, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0003">3h</a>. The Nyquist plots revealed a distinctive semicircular feature within the high-frequency domain, complemented by a linear gradient in the low-frequency range. In this regard, the MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs material exhibited a comparatively diminished semicircle and a steeper linear slope in contrast to the MnSe@HCTs, underscoring lower charge transfer resistance (Rct) and expedited ion diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0045_R_d44637599e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - ACS Nano">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This distinction can be attributed to the elevated conductivity and the distinctive yolk-shell architecture inherent to the MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs. Subsequently, the cyclic performance of these materials was delineated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0003">3i</a>. MnSe@HCTs (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3f</a>, Supporting Information) showcased enduring cycling stability over a span exceeding 1000 cycles, maintaining a specific capacity of 215&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Impressively, the MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs showed even higher capacity, reaching 408.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the same current density, while concurrently exhibiting remarkable cycling stability across &gt;1000 cycles. The HCTs showed a high capacity of 152&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which contributed to the enhanced cycling stability of the composite (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a>, Supporting Information). This result presented a significant enhancement in cycling stability, symbolizing the efficacy of the novel MnSe/ZnSe@HCT architecture.</p>
         
         <p>The MnSe@HCTs (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) and MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0004">4</a>) anodes were investigated using CV galvanostatic charge/discharge curves, and cyclic performance to assess their practical applications in KIBs. In the CV tests (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0004">4a</a>), MnSe@HCTs revealed distinct redox peaks signifying reversible intercalation of potassium ions. The MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs heterojunctions exhibited enhanced electrochemical performance characterized by increased peak intensity and reduced potential hysteresis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0046_R_d44637599e1104" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These improvements suggest superior kinetics and heightened electrochemical activity. galvanostatic charge/discharge measurements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0004">4b</a>) further presented the favorable charge–discharge behavior of both materials. MnSe@HCTs (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>b,Supporting Information) exhibited a specific capacity of 145&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, while MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs demonstrated an elevated specific capacity of 422&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, notable rate capabilities were demonstrated across various current rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0004">4c</a>), accompanied by consistently stable capacities. Particularly noteworthy was the high capacity of ≈220&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> achieved at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating efficient Na<sup>+</sup> transport and robust electronic conductivity within the material. Furthermore, the capacity recovered seamlessly when the current density returned back to 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, presenting the excellent reversibility of the battery.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0047_R_d44637599e1135" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The good electrical conductivity of HCTs contributed to the good rate performance of the KIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5c</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0004">4d</a> depicted the relationship between current density and scan rate, revealing the nonlinear behavior of diffusion-controlled reactions and capacitive processes in potassium storage. The advantageous properties of MnSe/ZnSe@HCT were further revealed through EIS analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0004">4e</a>). These heterojunctions exhibited reduced charge transfer resistance (Rct) and accelerated ion diffusion due to the inherent superior conductivity and unique yolk-shell structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0048_R_d44637599e1149" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Nano">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs presented superior cycling stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0004">4f</a>) by retaining a capacity of 158&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, in contrast to MnSe@HCTs with capacity decay and degradation over cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4f</a>, Supporting Information). The HCTs presented a high-capacity retention of 72% after 1000 cycles at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which attributed to the improved cycling stability of the MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5d</a>, Supporting Information). The heterojunction structures of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs played an important role in improving the performance of potassium-ion batteries.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307747-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cf76cece-aefb-4300-9159-dc4fe34d8e42/smll202307747-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cf76cece-aefb-4300-9159-dc4fe34d8e42/smll202307747-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0c7e20cb-8916-4b95-bf16-006097bfe20c/smll202307747-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cf76cece-aefb-4300-9159-dc4fe34d8e42/smll202307747-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307747-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307747" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves and b) galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs in K-ion batteries for the first five cycles. c) Rate performance of MnSe@HCTs in K-ion batteries. d) CV curves at different scan rates. e) EIS image of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs and MnSe@HCTs. f) The cycling stability of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs in K-ion batteries.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The charge density difference depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0005">5a</a>, illustrating the interaction between ZnSe and MnSe, which resulted in the transfer of 0.54 electrons from ZnSe to MnSe at the interface. This electron transfer generates a higher electric potential in ZnSe relative to MnSe. To assess the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption capacity, the modeling of Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption structures on MnSe/ZnSe heterojunctions were conducted and presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0005">5b</a>. The combination of selenium vacancies and heterostructure significantly enhances the adsorption capacity of Na<sup>+</sup>. Moreover, the charge density difference plots for MnSe/ZnSe heterojunctions in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0005">5c</a> reveal denser charge accumulation regions surrounding the vacancies. This observation presented a greater loss of electrons by Na<sup>+</sup> ions, confirming the beneficial role of vacancies in strengthening the binding of Na<sup>+</sup> with the composite material. The total density of states (TDOS) presented that the presence of selenium vacancies and defect states in MnSe/ZnSe heterojunctions, resulting in a reduced bandgap (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307747-fig-0005">5d</a>). MnSe/ZnSe heterojunctions exhibited an elevated Fermi level, forming N-type degenerate semiconductors with properties approaching those of metals. This alteration significantly enhanced electrical conductivity of materials. Hence, these findings demonstrate the importance of selenium vacancies and heterostructures in optimizing the electronic properties of materials and their potential for advanced applications. These included improved ion diffusion and intercalation, increased electrical conductivity, and minimized electrode volume expansion.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307747-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/62d7a09d-0c72-4aa4-bce1-051c5b13cdd0/smll202307747-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/62d7a09d-0c72-4aa4-bce1-051c5b13cdd0/smll202307747-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/65f28a7b-9339-4492-890a-87ffc4bdcf4a/smll202307747-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/62d7a09d-0c72-4aa4-bce1-051c5b13cdd0/smll202307747-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307747-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307747-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, the redistribution of electrons at the phase boundary increases the adsorption sites for Na and K ions. MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs exhibited an excellent capacity of 475&nbsp;mA&nbsp;hg at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and an ultra-stable cycle capacity of 408.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles. The high-capacity retention of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs based anode can be attributed to the yolk-shell structure and efficient electron transfer between the active material and HCTs. As electrode in KIBs, MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs delivered a specific capacity of 422&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and kept a coulombic  efficiency of 99% after 1000 cycles. The optimized MnSe/ZnSe yolk-shell structure can provide abundant interfacial boundary/selenide in HCTs substrate to provide a faster Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> pathway, reducing ions diffusion barrier and increasing electronic conductivity. Through comprehensive experimental investigations, our study has introduced a MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs anode material that demonstrates exceptional efficacy in the realm of sodium and potassium storage. This achievement serves as a catalyst for inspiring forthcoming research endeavors focused on conversion-type electrode materials, thereby propelling the advancement of SIBs and KIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307747-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307747-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202307747-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202307747-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202307747-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202307747-sec-0070-title"> Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>Manganese sulfate (MnSO<sub>4</sub> H<sub>2</sub>O, 99.0%) and Zinc nitrate (Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, 99.0%) were purchased from Nanjing Chemical Reagent Co. Ltd. Selenium powder (Se, 99.0%) and 2-Methylimidazole (C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>6</sub>N<sub>2</sub>,98%) were purchased from Guangzhou Chemical Reagent Factory. Polyvinylpyrrolidone (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>9</sub>NO)<sub>n</sub> was purchased from Tianli Chemical Agent Co., Ltd. The above-mentioned reagents were of analytical grade and employed without subsequent purification.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202307747-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202307747-sec-0080-title"> Material Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p><i>Preparation of MnSe@HCTs</i>: First, 0.338&nbsp;g MnSO<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O and 0.316&nbsp;g Se powder were dissolved in the 44&nbsp;mL DI water, stirring ≈10&nbsp;min to obtain a uniform solution. Subsequently, 0.5&nbsp;g pre-treated dandelion tomentums with 20&nbsp;mL ethanol were added to this solution and ultrasonicated for 20&nbsp;min. Then, 16&nbsp;mL N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O to the above mixed solution was added, stirring for 50&nbsp;min to completely react. The procured mixture solution was treated in a 100&nbsp;mL Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave at 180&nbsp;°C for 48&nbsp;h. The as-prepared sample was placed in the center of a tubular furnace to further annealed reaction. In the atmosphere of argon, the ramp rate of 2&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> was set to attain 800&nbsp;°C and maintained for 2&nbsp;h. The obtained material was denoted as MnSe@HCTs.</p>
                  
                  <p><i>Preparation of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs</i>: First, 0.258&nbsp;g MnSe@HCTs, 1.063&nbsp;g Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O and 0.5&nbsp;g PVP were dissolved in 90&nbsp;mL methanol. After stirring for 48&nbsp;h, 2.462&nbsp;g 2-methylimidazole and 90&nbsp;mL methanol were slowly added in mixture solution and agitated intensely for 30&nbsp;min. Then, the obtained samples by centrifugal collection were rinsed by DI water and dried in 65&nbsp;°C vacuum overnight. The as-prepared sample was placed in the center of a tubular furnace to further calcinate treatment. In the atmosphere of argon, the ramp rate of 2&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> was set to attain 800&nbsp;°C and maintained for 2&nbsp;h. The obtained material was named as MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202307747-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202307747-sec-0090-title"> Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The phase compositions and crystal structures of the samples were identified by X-ray diffraction (XRD, Rigaku TTR III) with Cu Kα radiation (<i>λ</i>&nbsp;= 1.5418 Å). The X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (XPS, Thermo ESCALAB 250) was used to detect the chemical states of the surface elements. The morphological attributes of the specimens were assessed through the utilization of scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Hitachi S-4800). For an examination of the material microstructures, transmission electron microscopy (TEM, FEI Tecnai G2 F20 S-TWIN) was employed. The elemental dispersion was ascertained using energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS). Electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) spectroscopy was employed to explore the materials that have unpaired electrons using Germany Bruker EMXPLUS instrument. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) of the substance was conducted utilizing the Shimadzu TGA-50H instrument under ambient air conditions. Raman spectrometer (Jobin-Yvon HR800) was applied to record the Raman spectra of samples. Fourier spectrometer (PerkinElmer SP-100) was performed to acquire the infrared spectrum of MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202307747-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202307747-sec-0100-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>For the preparation of the electrode, first, electrochemical active material (MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs), Super P and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) were mixed in the weight ratio of 8:1:1. After stirring in n-methyl pyrrolidone (NMP) for 4&nbsp;h, the mixed slurry was painted on current collector (Cu foil) and dried in 65&nbsp;°C vacuum overnight. The desiccated foil underwent segmentation to procure a functional electrode, possessing a diameter measuring 12&nbsp;mm. In the cathode, the active material loading reached an approximate magnitude of 2.4&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. Coin-type CR2032 cells were meticulously assembled within the confines of an argon-filled glove box. The assemblage of half cells also took place within the controlled argon atmosphere of the glove box. These half cells were subsequently employed for the assessment of electrochemical characteristics, utilizing MnSe/ZnSe@HCTs as the functional electrode, a Whatman GF/D separator, and Na/K metal functioning as the anode. The electrolyte solution employed was a composite of ethylene carbonate (EC), diethyl carbonate (DEC), and ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC), in a volumetric ratio of 1:1:1. The electrolyte was prepared using either 1.0&nbsp;M NaClO<sub>4</sub> or 0.8&nbsp;M KPF<sub>6</sub>. To conduct galvanostatic discharge/charge measurements within the potential window of 0.1–3&nbsp;V, NEWARE battery testers (CT-3008&nbsp;W) were employed. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) spanning the potential range of 0.1–3&nbsp;V, as well as electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), were performed using an Autolab electrochemical workstation (PGSTAT 302N).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202307747-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202307747-sec-0110-title"> Statistical Analysis</h4>
                  
                  <p>The spin-polarized density functional theory calculations were conducted using the Vienna ab initio simulation package (VASP). To account for van der Waals (vdW) interactions, the D3 correction approach (DFT-D3) was employed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0049_R_d44637599e1376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Phys. Rev. B">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The exchange-functional was treated with the Perdew-Burke-Ernzerhof (PBE) functional within the framework of the generalized gradient approximation (GGA).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0050_R_d44637599e1381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Core-valence interactions were represented using the projector-augmented wave (PAW) approach.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307747-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307747-bib-0051_R_d44637599e1386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Chem. Phys.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Electron smearing was implemented using the Gaussian smearing method with a width of 0.05&nbsp;eV. The plane wave basis expansion was truncated at an energy cutoff of 500&nbsp;eV. For Brillouin-zone integration, a Monkhorst-Pack K-mesh with a K-point separation of 0.04 Å<sup>−1</sup> was utilized. Structural optimization was achieved by minimizing the forces on each ion through the conjugate gradient algorithm until they reached a convergence threshold of 0.02&nbsp;eV/Å. Self-consistent calculations employed a convergence energy threshold of 10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
